At this degree of extension, critics argue, the term loses its analytical bite: if every narrator is potentially unreliable by virtue of being a perspective, then "unreliable narrator" names a universal condition rather than a distinctive narratological category, and the term becomes descriptively inert.

The reintroduction of wolves to Yellowstone National Park in 1995 produced a textbook example. As wolf populations recovered, elk began to avoid overgrazing riverbank vegetation — not because their numbers declined substantially, but because they modified their behavior to avoid areas where predation risk was high. The resulting recovery of riparian plants stabilized stream banks, changed water flow patterns, and increased habitat for multiple other species. Geomorphologists — scientists who study landforms — found that the wolves had, in effect, altered the physical geography of the park.

     D) A well-known ecological theory is challenged by new evidence from a single case study.

     Correct Answer: A

     Correct Answer Explanation:
     * Choice A is correct because the passage opens by defining "trophic cascade," then uses the Yellowstone wolf reintroduction as a concrete illustration of the concept, extending it to the surprising finding that apex predators can alter the physical landscape — the structure is definition followed by illustrative case that deepens the concept's implications.

     False Answer Explanations:
     * Choice B is incorrect because the passage presents both positive and negative views of the technique without defending one over the other; it is not an apology for the novelist's choice.
     * Choice C is incorrect because the passage does not describe two schools of criticism with contrasting methods; it presents two reader responses to one technique in one novel.
     * Choice D is incorrect because the passage explicitly declines to resolve the debate, framing the question as one about readers' expectations rather than arriving at a definitive judgment.

     -----------------------------------------------------------------

     QUESTION 38
     Passage:
     The sociologist argued that the discourse of "meritocracy" functions ideologically not despite its partial accuracy but because of it. In societies with genuine upward mobility, the existence of individuals who rise through talent and effort is highly visible; their stories become available as templates, confirming that the system rewards merit. What this visibility conceals is the structural role of luck, inherited advantage, and network effects in determining outcomes for the majority. The merit narrative is thus not simply a lie but something more insidious: a selectively true account that makes the full picture harder to see by supplying vivid evidence for a partial conclusion.

     Prompt:
     Which choice best describes the overall structure of the text?

     Options:
     A) An ideological claim is introduced, the mechanism by which it operates is explained, and the source of its persuasive power and danger is identified.
     B) A sociological theory is described, and a series of empirical studies confirming its predictions is presented.
     C) A common argument for meritocracy is outlined and then refuted through a direct examination of economic inequality data.
     D) Two competing sociological accounts of social mobility are introduced and assessed for their explanatory power.

     Correct Answer: A

     Correct Answer Explanation:
     * Choice A is correct because the passage introduces the idea that meritocracy functions as ideology, explains the mechanism — visible success stories confirming a partial truth — and then identifies the source of its particular danger: it is not a simple lie but a selectively true account, which makes it more persuasive and harder to challenge than outright falsehood.

     False Answer Explanations:
     * Choice B is incorrect because the passage presents a theoretical argument without citing empirical studies; the structure is analytical, not data-driven.
     * Choice C is incorrect because the passage does not present economic inequality data; the argument operates at the level of social epistemology — how narratives shape perception — not statistics.
     * Choice D is incorrect because only one sociological account is developed; no competing theory is introduced for comparison.

     QUESTION 39
     Passage:
     There is something paradoxical about the demand, frequently made of marginalized writers, that their work be "universal." The demand sounds like an invitation — write beyond your particular experience, speak to all of humanity — but it operates, critics have argued, as a form of exclusion. To be legible as universal, a work must conform to aesthetic and thematic norms established by the dominant culture, which has historically centered certain kinds of subjects, settings, and concerns as default human experience while treating others as niche. The demand for universality thus functions as a gatekeeping mechanism: it admits writers whose particularity is invisible — because it matches the norm — while requiring others to translate, dilute, or transcend their specificity as the price of admission.

     Prompt:
     Which choice best describes the overall structure of the text?

     Options:
     A) A seemingly inclusive demand is introduced, its apparent meaning is distinguished from its actual function, and the mechanism by which it enforces exclusion is explained.
     B) A literary concept is defined and then applied to a series of specific works to evaluate their success.
     C) Two competing views of universality in literature are presented and the more defensible one is endorsed.
     D) A historical account of how aesthetic norms developed in Western literature is provided.

     Correct Answer: A

     Correct Answer Explanation:
     * Choice A is correct because the passage opens by identifying the demand for universality and its surface meaning, immediately complicates it by revealing its exclusionary function, and then explains the specific mechanism — conformity to dominant aesthetic norms — by which it enforces that exclusion. The structure moves from surface meaning to hidden function to structural explanation.

     False Answer Explanations:
     * Choice B is incorrect because the passage does not apply the concept to specific literary works; it develops a theoretical argument about how the demand functions.
     * Choice C is incorrect because no second view of universality is presented — the passage develops one critical perspective rather than weighing two competing ones.
     * Choice D is incorrect because the passage is not historical in its approach; it analyzes a current critical problem rather than tracing the development of aesthetic norms over time.

     QUESTION 40
     Passage:
     The concept of "late style" — the idea that major artists, facing mortality, produce work characterized by a distinctive intractability, difficulty, or refusal of resolution — has proved generative in music and literary criticism since Edward Said's elaboration of it. What is rarely acknowledged is the concept's inherent unfalsifiability. If a late work is difficult, it confirms the theory; if it is serene or reconciliatory — as Verdi's Falstaff or Shakespeare's late romances might be characterized — critics simply reclassify the serenity as a more subtle form of resistance. The theory expands to accommodate whatever the artist produces, which means it explains everything and therefore, in a strict sense, explains nothing.

     Prompt:
     Which choice best describes the overall structure of the text?

     Options:
     A) A generative critical concept is introduced, and then its methodological unfalsifiability — and the interpretive mechanism that produces it — is identified and critiqued.
     B) Two competing accounts of late style are presented, and the passage argues for the superiority of one.
     C) A theory's historical development is traced and its influence on two art forms is assessed.
     D) An apparently compelling critical framework is introduced and then defended against the charge that it is circular.

     Correct Answer: A

     Correct Answer Explanation:
     * Choice A is correct because the passage acknowledges the productivity of the "late style" concept before identifying the specific methodological problem: the theory cannot be falsified because its terms are redefined to accommodate any counter-evidence. The final sentence draws the logical conclusion that an unfalsifiable theory lacks genuine explanatory power — the structure moves from acknowledging value to exposing a fundamental epistemic flaw.

     False Answer Explanations:
     * Choice B is incorrect because no competing account of late style is introduced; the passage develops a single critical perspective on the concept's weakness.
     * Choice C is incorrect because the passage does not trace the theory's history in detail or assess its influence across art forms — it focuses on a structural flaw in the concept.
     * Choice D is incorrect because the passage does not defend the concept against criticism — it is itself the source of the critique. The passage argues the theory is circular, not that it isn't.

     QUESTION 1
     Passage 1:
     Regular aerobic exercise has well-documented benefits for cardiovascular health. Studies consistently show that individuals who exercise at least three times per week have lower rates of heart disease, reduced blood pressure, and improved cholesterol levels compared to sedentary individuals. Doctors widely recommend moderate aerobic activity as one of the most effective preventive health measures available.

     Passage 2:
     While exercise is often promoted as a cure-all, its benefits depend heavily on an individual's baseline health. For people already managing chronic conditions such as joint disease or heart failure, unsupervised vigorous exercise can cause harm rather than benefit. Medical supervision is essential before beginning any new exercise program, particularly for older adults.

     Prompt:
     Based on the texts, how would the author of Passage 2 most likely respond to the recommendation made in Passage 1?

     Options:
     A) By arguing that aerobic exercise has no proven cardiovascular benefits
     B) By agreeing with the recommendation but adding that it should be applied with care depending on individual health circumstances
     C) By suggesting that exercise should be replaced entirely by medication for heart disease prevention
     D) By claiming that only younger adults benefit from aerobic exercise

     Correct Answer: B

     Correct Answer Explanation:
     * Choice B is correct because the author of Passage 2 does not deny the value of exercise — they caution that it must be applied carefully based on individual health status and with medical supervision, which is a qualification rather than a rejection of Passage 1's recommendation.

     False Answer Explanations:
     * Choice A is incorrect because Passage 2 never disputes that exercise has cardiovascular benefits; it raises concerns about unsupervised exercise for people with specific conditions.
     * Choice C is incorrect because Passage 2 makes no mention of medication as an alternative; its concern is about supervised versus unsupervised exercise.
     * Choice D is incorrect because Passage 2 specifically mentions older adults as requiring extra care, not as being excluded from benefit — it does not claim age negates the value of exercise.

     QUESTION 2
     Passage 1:
     Zoos play a vital role in wildlife conservation. By maintaining breeding populations of endangered species, they serve as insurance against extinction. Many animals that would have vanished from the wild entirely — including the Arabian oryx and the California condor — have been saved through zoo-based breeding programs and reintroduction efforts.

     Passage 2:
     Zoos, however well-intentioned, cannot substitute for the preservation of wild habitats. Animals kept in captivity often suffer from stress, limited space, and inability to perform natural behaviors. Conservation funding directed toward zoo operations might achieve greater impact if redirected to habitat protection and anti-poaching efforts in the regions where endangered species actually live.

     Prompt:
     Which of the following best describes the relationship between the two passages?

     Options:
     A) Passage 2 fully agrees with Passage 1 and adds supporting evidence.
     B) Passage 2 challenges Passage 1's claim by questioning zoos' effectiveness and proposing an alternative approach.
     C) Passage 2 focuses on a different subject than Passage 1.
     D) Passage 2 provides historical context for the argument made in Passage 1.

     Correct Answer: B

     Correct Answer Explanation:
     * Choice B is correct because Passage 2 directly challenges the conservation value of zoos argued in Passage 1 — questioning both animal welfare and funding efficiency — and proposes habitat preservation as a more effective alternative.

     False Answer Explanations:
     * Choice A is incorrect because Passage 2 challenges rather than supports Passage 1; it raises objections to zoo-based conservation.
     * Choice C is incorrect because both passages address the same subject: the role of zoos in wildlife conservation.
     * Choice D is incorrect because Passage 2 is argumentative, not historical — it makes claims about what conservation funding should prioritize, not about the past.

     QUESTION 3
     Passage 1:
     Reading fiction develops empathy. When readers inhabit the perspectives of characters whose lives differ from their own, they practice imagining how others think and feel. Researchers have found that people who read literary fiction score higher on tests measuring the ability to understand others' mental states — a skill known as theory of mind.

     Passage 2:
     Books of all kinds expand the mind. Whether through biography, history, science writing, or fiction, reading exposes people to new ideas, perspectives, and ways of understanding the world. The habit of regular reading is one of the strongest predictors of intellectual curiosity and openness to new experiences.

     Prompt:
     How do the two passages relate to each other?

     Options:
     A) Passage 1 makes a specific claim about one benefit of fiction that is consistent with the broader argument of Passage 2.
     B) Passage 1 contradicts the main point of Passage 2 by focusing only on fiction.
     C) Passage 2 directly refutes the research findings cited in Passage 1.
     D) The two passages argue for completely opposite conclusions about the value of reading.

     Correct Answer: A

     Correct Answer Explanation:
     * Choice A is correct because Passage 1's specific argument — that literary fiction builds empathy and theory of mind — is a particular example of the broader claim in Passage 2 that reading expands the mind and fosters openness; the two passages are compatible and complementary.

     False Answer Explanations:
     * Choice B is incorrect because focusing on one genre (fiction) does not contradict the broader argument — it supports it with a specific example.
     * Choice C is incorrect because Passage 2 does not mention or challenge any research findings; it makes a general argument about reading as a whole.
     * Choice D is incorrect because both passages argue that reading is beneficial — they differ only in scope, not in conclusion.

     QUESTION 4
     Passage 1:
     Social media platforms have made it easier than ever for people to stay connected with friends and family. Users can share life updates, coordinate events, and maintain relationships across geographic distances that would have made regular contact nearly impossible in earlier generations. For many people, platforms like these are a genuine source of community and belonging.

     Passage 2:
     Despite their surface sociability, social media platforms frequently leave users feeling more isolated than before. Studies have linked heavy social media use to increased feelings of loneliness and lower life satisfaction, particularly among young people. Curated highlight reels and the pressure of public performance create a form of connection that is wide but shallow.

     Prompt:
     Based on the texts, the authors of Passage 1 and Passage 2 would most likely disagree about which of the following?

     Options:
     A) Whether social media platforms are used by large numbers of people
     B) Whether the connections formed through social media contribute positively to users' wellbeing
     C) Whether geographic distance makes friendship more difficult to maintain
     D) Whether young people use social media more than older adults

     * Choice A is incorrect because neither passage disputes that social media has a large user base; that is an unstated shared assumption.
     * Choice C is incorrect because both passages would agree that geographic distance creates challenges for maintaining relationships — Passage 1 presents social media as a solution and Passage 2 does not contradict that premise.

     QUESTION 5
     Passage 1:

By making books affordable and widely available, Gutenberg's press broke the monopoly that clergy and scholars had held over literacy and learning. Ideas could now travel faster and farther than ever before, laying the groundwork for the Renaissance and the Scientific Revolution.

     Passage 2:
     The internet has done for the twenty-first century what the printing press did for the fifteenth. Both technologies dramatically lowered the cost of sharing information and disrupted existing gatekeepers of knowledge. Just as the press challenged the authority of the church and universities, the internet has challenged the authority of established media, governments, and academic institutions.

     Prompt:
     Which of the following best describes how the author of Passage 2 uses the information in Passage 1?

     Options:
     A) As a counterexample that weakens the argument about the internet
     B) As a historical parallel to strengthen the claim about the internet's significance
     C) As evidence that the internet is less transformative than the printing press
     D) As a source of data about fifteenth-century literacy rates

     Correct Answer: B

     Correct Answer Explanation:
     * Choice B is correct because Passage 2 explicitly draws a comparison between the printing press and the internet — using the historical impact of the press, as described in Passage 1, to frame and elevate the argument about the internet's similarly transformative significance.

     False Answer Explanations:
     * Choice A is incorrect because Passage 2 treats the printing press as a supportive parallel, not as a counterexample that undermines its claim.
     * Choice C is incorrect because Passage 2 presents the two technologies as comparable in significance, not as evidence that the internet falls short.
     * Choice D is incorrect because Passage 1 does not provide data about literacy rates, and Passage 2 does not treat it as a source of statistics.

Before we spend billions sending probes to distant planets, we should ensure that basic human needs are met on this one.

     A) Passage 2 agrees with Passage 1 that space exploration has produced valuable technologies.
     B) Passage 2 disputes the scientific value of space exploration entirely.
     C) Passage 2 accepts that space exploration has value but questions whether it should be a funding priority given unmet human needs.

     Passage 2:

For students whose goal is rapid English proficiency for academic and professional purposes, structured immersion can be the most efficient path, particularly when delivered with strong instructional support.

     QUESTION 11
     Passage 1:
     Standardized testing provides an objective measure of student achievement that is immune to the subjective biases inherent in teacher grading. A student's grade from a particular teacher reflects not just their knowledge but also factors like class participation, effort, and the teacher's personal impressions. Standardized tests, by contrast, apply the same criteria uniformly across all students, providing a more reliable basis for comparison.

     Passage 2:
     The claim that standardized tests are objective is itself misleading. Tests are designed by humans who make choices about what knowledge to assess, how to phrase questions, and which skills to prioritize — choices that inevitably reflect certain cultural assumptions and educational values. Students from backgrounds that match those assumptions tend to perform better, not because they know more, but because the test was implicitly designed around their experience.

     Prompt:
     Based on the texts, how would the author of Passage 2 most likely respond to the author of Passage 1's claim that standardized tests apply "the same criteria uniformly"?

     Options:
     A) By agreeing that uniform criteria make tests fairer than teacher grades
     B) By arguing that uniform criteria are valuable but need to be updated more frequently
     C) By contending that applying criteria uniformly does not make those criteria free from cultural bias
     D) By suggesting that standardized tests should be replaced by student portfolios

     Correct Answer: C

     Correct Answer Explanation:
     * Choice C is correct because Passage 2's argument is specifically that uniform application of criteria does not eliminate the bias embedded in the criteria themselves — the test can apply its standards consistently while those standards still favor students from particular backgrounds.

     False Answer Explanations:
     * Choice A is incorrect because Passage 2 rejects the premise that uniform criteria produce fairness; agreeing with Passage 1 here would contradict Passage 2's central argument.
     * Choice B is incorrect because Passage 2's objection is structural — the criteria embed cultural assumptions — not a complaint about outdatedness or frequency of revision.
     * Choice D is incorrect because while this might follow from Passage 2's argument, it is not a claim Passage 2 makes; the passage critiques standardized tests but does not propose a specific replacement.

     QUESTION 13

     While it is true that print journalism has declined, new forms of local accountability journalism have emerged to fill some of the gap.      False Answer Explanations:
     * Choice A is incorrect because Passage 2 does not accept Passage 1's framing as fully correct; it explicitly argues the situation is less dire than Passage 1 implies.
     * Choice C is incorrect because Passage 2 does not address the quality of print newspaper reporting — its argument is about whether accountability journalism as a function has disappeared, not about how good traditional papers were.

     QUESTION 14
     Passage 1:

Research has linked moderate coffee consumption to reduced risk of several diseases, including Parkinson's and type 2 diabetes. For the vast majority of people, a few cups of coffee a day poses no meaningful health risk.

     The safety of caffeine consumption depends heavily on individual variation. Genetic differences in how people metabolize caffeine mean that a dose that is harmless for one person can significantly elevate cardiovascular risk for another.      False Answer Explanations:
     * Choice A is incorrect because Passage 1 argues the opposite — that caffeine is safe and mildly beneficial for most people.
     * Choice B is incorrect because Passage 2 directly challenges this view, and even Passage 1 qualifies its claims to apply to "most" adults rather than claiming universal safety.
     * Choice D is incorrect because Passage 1 cites research linking coffee to reduced disease risk, meaning it explicitly identifies health benefits.

     QUESTION 15
     Passage 1:

Employees who witness a manager speaking one way to them and a completely different way to executives often lose trust in that manager's authenticity and wonder which version represents the person's true values.

     Prompt:
     Based on the texts, the authors of Passage 1 and Passage 2 would most likely disagree about whether:

     Options:
     A) Leaders should communicate clearly with all audiences
     B) Adapting communication style to different audiences is compatible with authentic leadership
     C) Trust is important in relationships between managers and employees
     D) Leadership can be developed through practice and training

     Correct Answer: B

     Correct Answer Explanation:
     * Choice B is correct because Passage 1 treats audience-adaptive communication as a hallmark of excellent leadership, while Passage 2 argues that dramatically varying one's style undermines authenticity and trust — they directly disagree about whether adaptation is a strength or a liability.

     False Answer Explanations:
     * Choice A is incorrect because both authors would agree that clarity is important; their disagreement is about whether style adaptation serves or undermines effective communication.
     * Choice C is incorrect because both authors value trust — Passage 2 explicitly invokes it as a reason to avoid style-switching, and Passage 1's interest in clear communication implicitly values the trust it builds.
     * Choice D is incorrect because neither passage addresses whether leadership skills can be trained or developed; that question is outside the scope of both texts.

     QUESTION 16
     Passage 1:
     Ancient Rome's system of roads was one of its most consequential engineering achievements. Spanning over 250,000 miles at its peak, the network allowed armies to move rapidly, merchants to trade across vast distances, and administrators to govern an empire that stretched from Britain to Mesopotamia. The roads were so well engineered that many remained in use for centuries after Rome's fall.

     Passage 2:
     The Roman road network, impressive as it was, served primarily military and administrative purposes — the needs of the state rather than the general population. Ordinary Romans, particularly those in rural areas, rarely benefited directly from the major roads, which were designed for the movement of legions and official messengers. The roads' longevity reflects their military-grade construction, not their accessibility to common people.

     Options:
     A) By arguing that the Roman roads were not as well-engineered as Passage 1 claims
     B) By questioning whether the Roman Empire was actually as large as Passage 1 suggests
     C) By challenging the claim that the roads benefited Roman society broadly, arguing they mainly served state and military functions
     D) By suggesting that the roads were built by enslaved people rather than Roman engineers

     Correct Answer: C

     Correct Answer Explanation:
     * Choice C is correct because Passage 1 presents the road network as a broad achievement benefiting armies, merchants, and administrators alike, while Passage 2 narrows that picture — arguing the roads served state and military needs primarily, leaving rural and ordinary Romans largely outside their benefits.

     False Answer Explanations:
     * Choice A is incorrect because Passage 2 explicitly acknowledges the roads were "military-grade" in construction quality; it does not dispute the engineering.
     * Choice B is incorrect because neither passage disputes the size of the Roman Empire, and Passage 2 does not raise that question.
     * Choice D is incorrect because labor practices are not mentioned in either passage; Passage 2's critique focuses on who benefited from the roads, not who built them.

     QUESTION 18
     Passage 1:
     Nuclear energy should be part of any serious strategy for reducing carbon emissions. Modern nuclear plants produce enormous amounts of electricity with virtually no direct greenhouse gas emissions during operation. As countries seek to decarbonize their grids, dismissing nuclear power on the basis of fears rooted in decades-old accidents is shortsighted.

     Passage 2:
     The appeal of nuclear energy as a climate solution is understandable but overstated. The true carbon cost of nuclear includes the energy required to mine uranium, enrich fuel, and construct plants — processes that are far from emission-free. Furthermore, the economics of nuclear power have grown increasingly unfavorable compared to rapidly falling costs of solar and wind energy.

     B) Nuclear power plants produce emissions during their operation
     C) Nuclear energy represents a cost-effective and genuinely low-carbon solution to climate change

     * Choice B is incorrect because Passage 1 actually specifies "no direct greenhouse gas emissions during operation," and Passage 2 does not dispute that specific claim — it raises the full-cycle emissions instead.

     QUESTION 20
     Passage 1:
     Travel is one of the most transformative experiences available to a person. Encountering unfamiliar cultures, foods, languages, and landscapes forces travelers to question their assumptions and broaden their understanding of what it means to live a human life. People who travel widely tend to be more empathetic, more flexible in their thinking, and more comfortable with ambiguity.

When travel is held up as a uniquely broadening experience, it implicitly devalues the intellectual and empathetic growth available through reading, conversation, art, and community engagement — pursuits accessible to those for whom travel is not.

     * Choice A is incorrect because environmental costs are not mentioned in Passage 2; its critique is about access and the implicit devaluing of non-travel-based growth.

     QUESTION 21
     Passage 1:
     Psychological research on "ego depletion" suggests that willpower is a finite resource that diminishes with use. Studies in the early 2000s appeared to show that people who exerted self-control on one task subsequently performed worse on unrelated self-control tasks, as if drawing from the same limited mental reservoir. The finding was influential in popular psychology and was widely used to recommend behaviors such as making important decisions earlier in the day.

     Passage 2:
     The ego depletion effect has failed to replicate consistently in large-scale studies. A major pre-registered replication effort involving multiple laboratories found no significant depletion effect, and subsequent meta-analyses have suggested that early positive results may reflect publication bias toward studies with dramatic findings. Some researchers now argue that willpower is better understood as a motivational state influenced by beliefs and expectations rather than a physiological resource that runs out.

     Prompt:
     Based on the texts, how does the evidence presented in Passage 2 affect the recommendations described in Passage 1?

     Options:
     A) It confirms that people should make important decisions early in the day to conserve willpower.
     B) It undermines the empirical basis for those recommendations by casting doubt on the depletion model they were derived from.
     C) It suggests the recommendations are correct but were derived from the wrong explanation.
     D) It strengthens the case for ego depletion by providing additional replications of the original findings.

     Correct Answer: B

     Correct Answer Explanation:
     * Choice B is correct because the practical recommendations in Passage 1 — such as making decisions early — were based on the ego depletion model; Passage 2 reports that this model has failed to replicate and may reflect publication bias, which directly undermines the empirical foundation those recommendations rest on.

     False Answer Explanations:
     * Choice A is incorrect because Passage 2 challenges rather than confirms the ego depletion model on which the early-decision recommendation was based.
     * Choice C is incorrect because Passage 2 does not suggest the recommendations happen to be correct for different reasons — it questions the entire depletion framework without validating the behavioral advice.
     * Choice D is incorrect because Passage 2 describes failed replications and questions the original findings, the opposite of strengthening the original case.

     QUESTION 22
     Passage 1:
     The success of the Scandinavian social democratic model — combining high taxes, generous welfare states, and robust labor protections with strong economic performance — demonstrates that equality and prosperity are not in tension. Nordic countries consistently rank among the wealthiest, happiest, and most innovative in the world, suggesting that the trade-off between redistribution and growth is largely a myth.

     Passage 2:
     Attributing Nordic prosperity to social democratic policy requires controlling for a range of confounding factors that proponents of the model often overlook. Nordic countries had relatively high levels of social trust, low corruption, and strong institutional quality before the welfare state expanded significantly. It is plausible that these pre-existing conditions, rather than welfare policies themselves, are the primary drivers of prosperity — and that similar policies transplanted to countries without those conditions might produce different results.

     Prompt:
     Based on the texts, the author of Passage 2 would most likely argue that the author of Passage 1's reasoning:

     Options:
     A) Correctly identifies a causal relationship between welfare policy and prosperity
     B) Mistakes correlation for causation by ignoring pre-existing conditions that may explain both policy choices and economic outcomes
     C) Relies on inaccurate data about Nordic economic performance
     D) Overstates the generosity of Nordic welfare states compared to other European countries

     Correct Answer: B

     Correct Answer Explanation:
     * Choice B is correct because Passage 2's core methodological objection is that Passage 1 infers a causal relationship — welfare policies cause prosperity — without controlling for confounders like social trust and institutional quality that predate the welfare expansion and may explain both the policy choices and the outcomes.

     False Answer Explanations:
     * Choice A is incorrect because Passage 2 explicitly disputes whether the causal relationship Passage 1 assumes has been established.
     * Choice C is incorrect because Passage 2 does not question the accuracy of data about Nordic prosperity — it accepts that the countries are prosperous and questions the explanation.
     * Choice D is incorrect because Passage 2 does not compare Nordic welfare generosity to other European countries; its argument is about causal inference, not comparative policy scope.

     QUESTION 23
     Passage 1:
     The novelist argues in her essay that fiction's primary moral function is to cultivate the reader's imaginative identification with others.      Passage 2:
     Fiction's relationship to moral life is more complicated than the "empathy machine" model suggests. Research has shown that reading fiction does not reliably translate into more ethical behavior in practice — people can imaginatively inhabit a character's suffering while remaining unmoved in their actual moral conduct. The novel may expand the imaginative self without enlarging the ethical one.

     Options:
     A) Fiction reliably causes readers to behave more ethically in their daily lives
     B) Imaginative identification with fictional characters is something reading can produce
     C) The moral function of fiction is well understood and uncontroversial
     D) Fiction that does not aim to instruct has no moral value

     Correct Answer: B

Both passages grant that the imaginative experience occurs; they disagree about what it achieves morally.

     False Answer Explanations:
     * Choice A is incorrect because Passage 2 explicitly disputes the claim that fiction reliably translates into more ethical behavior; this is a point of disagreement, not agreement.
     * Choice C is incorrect because the very existence of the debate between the two passages demonstrates that fiction's moral function is contested.

     QUESTION 24
     Passage 1:
     The growing use of predictive algorithms in criminal sentencing represents a troubling erosion of judicial discretion. Algorithms trained on historical data encode past patterns of racial and socioeconomic disparity into future decisions. A judge guided by an algorithm assigning a high recidivism risk score to a defendant may be less likely to exercise the individualized judgment that justice requires.

     Passage 2:
     Critics of algorithmic sentencing tools overlook the documented inconsistency of purely human judicial discretion. Studies have shown that identical cases receive dramatically different sentences depending on the judge assigned, the time of day, and factors entirely unrelated to the merits of the case. A well-designed algorithm, applied consistently, may introduce less variation and potentially less bias than a system dependent entirely on individual human judgment.

     Prompt:
     Based on the texts, what is the central disagreement between the two authors?

     Options:
     A) Whether recidivism is a legitimate factor to consider in criminal sentencing
     B) Whether the primary risk to fair sentencing comes from algorithmic bias or from unchecked human judicial discretion
     C) Whether historical data can ever be used responsibly in a legal context
     D) Whether all judges display the same level of racial bias in sentencing decisions

     Correct Answer: B

     Correct Answer Explanation:
     * Choice B is correct because Passage 1's concern is that algorithms embed historical bias and undermine individualized justice, while Passage 2 argues that human judges are themselves inconsistent and biased — they identify the same goal (fair sentencing) but disagree about which system poses the greater threat to it.

     False Answer Explanations:
     * Choice A is incorrect because neither passage disputes whether recidivism is a relevant sentencing consideration; the dispute is about how it should be assessed.
     * Choice C is incorrect because neither passage makes a blanket claim about whether historical data can ever be used in legal contexts; the debate is more targeted than that.
     * Choice D is incorrect because Passage 2 does not address racial bias in individual judges — it focuses on inconsistency and case-irrelevant factors, not specifically on racial disparities.

     QUESTION 25
     Passage 1:
     Environmental philosopher Holmes Rolston III argues that wilderness has intrinsic value — value that exists independently of human experience or appreciation. A mountain range or an old-growth forest is not valuable because humans find it beautiful or useful; it is valuable in itself, as a complex system of life that has evolved over millions of years. Destroying such a place is a moral wrong regardless of whether any human being is harmed by or even aware of the loss.

     Passage 2:
     The concept of intrinsic value in nature is philosophically unstable. Value, critics argue, is relational: it exists in the encounter between a valuing subject and an object. A world with no minds in it would be neither beautiful nor ugly, neither meaningful nor meaningless — it would simply be. The case for preserving wilderness is better made on grounds of human benefit, cultural meaning, and intergenerational responsibility than on the contested claim that rocks and trees have value independent of any consciousness that registers them.

     Prompt:
     Based on the texts, the central philosophical disagreement between the two authors is about:

     Options:
     A) Whether wilderness areas should be protected by law
     B) Whether value can exist independently of a conscious mind that recognizes it
     C) Whether human beings are capable of experiencing nature's beauty
     D) Whether environmental philosophy is a legitimate academic discipline

     Correct Answer: B

     Correct Answer Explanation:
     * Choice B is correct because Passage 1 asserts that wilderness has intrinsic value — value independent of human perception — while Passage 2 argues that value is inherently relational and requires a valuing subject; the dispute is fundamentally about the ontological status of value itself.

     False Answer Explanations:
     * Choice A is incorrect because neither passage is primarily about legal protection; the disagreement is philosophical, not policy-based — and both authors likely support preservation, just for different reasons.
     * Choice C is incorrect because neither passage disputes that humans can experience nature's beauty; the disagreement is about whether value depends on that experience.
     * Choice D is incorrect because neither author questions whether environmental philosophy is legitimate; they are themselves engaged in it.

     QUESTION 26
     Passage 1:
     The decline of Latin as a living language should be understood as a cultural impoverishment rather than a natural evolution. Latin was a language of extraordinary precision, flexibility, and philosophical depth. Its decline — driven largely by political fragmentation and the rise of vernacular nationalism — severed European civilization from a shared intellectual heritage that had sustained continuity across centuries of political change.

     Passage 2:
     The death of Latin as a spoken language was not a loss but a liberation. The dominance of Latin across medieval Europe was inseparable from ecclesiastical and institutional power structures that excluded vernacular expression — the language of peasants, women, merchants, and the unlettered. The rise of vernacular literature did not fragment European civilization; it expanded who could participate in it.

     B) The Catholic Church played a positive role in preserving Latin as a language of scholarship
     C) The transition away from Latin as a dominant language had significant cultural and political consequences
     D) Vernacular languages are linguistically inferior to Latin

     * Choice C is correct because Passage 1 argues the decline of Latin had significant negative cultural consequences (severing a shared heritage), and Passage 2 argues it had significant positive consequences (expanding cultural participation) — both authors agree the transition was consequential; they simply disagree about whether those consequences were good or bad.

     * Choice A is incorrect because both authors treat the decline of Latin as culturally significant — they disagree about the valence of that significance, not whether it exists.
     * Choice B is incorrect because neither passage evaluates the Church's role in preserving Latin scholarship; that topic is not directly addressed by either author.

     QUESTION 27
     Passage 1:
     The growing movement to remove statues of historical figures associated with slavery, colonialism, or other forms of oppression reflects a healthy democratic impulse: communities have the right to decide whose memory they choose to honor in public space. Statues are not neutral historical records; they are acts of commemoration, and decisions about who is commemorated embody a community's values.

     Passage 2:
     Removing historical statues, however morally comprehensible, risks severing citizens from a complex past they need to understand rather than sanitize. The impulse to curate public space to reflect only approved values can slide toward a kind of historical amnesia. A more constructive approach would be to recontextualize statues — through accompanying text, counter-monuments, or museum relocation — rather than to erase their presence from the landscape.

     A) Historical statues should never be removed under any circumstances
     B) The decision about what to commemorate in public space is a genuinely important question with real cultural stakes
     C) Statues of all historical figures should be relocated to museums
     D) Citizens have no right to shape public commemorative landscapes

     Correct Answer: B

     Correct Answer Explanation:

     QUESTION 28
     Passage 1:
     The economist argues that universal basic income (UBI) — a regular unconditional cash transfer to all citizens — would reduce poverty and increase individual freedom by giving people the security to take risks, leave exploitative employment, and pursue education or creative work. Cash transfers, evidence from pilot programs suggests, are typically spent on necessities and productive investments rather than on frivolous consumption.

     Passage 2:
     UBI's appeal rests on an unduly individualistic conception of what people need from a social safety net. What the poor lack is not simply income but access to healthcare, childcare, housing, and education — services whose provision through markets is systematically inadequate. Replacing robust public services with a cash transfer risks dismantling the institutional infrastructure that delivers those services, leaving individuals with money but without the social goods money cannot reliably buy in unregulated markets.

     Prompt:
     Based on the texts, the author of Passage 2 would most likely argue that the proposal in Passage 1 risks:

     Options:
     A) Providing too small a cash transfer to make a meaningful difference in poverty rates
     B) Substituting a market-mediated solution for an institutional one, thereby dismantling social services that cash alone cannot replicate
     C) Creating inflation by increasing the money supply through universal payments
     D) Disincentivizing work by providing unconditional income to employed and unemployed people alike

     Correct Answer: B

     Correct Answer Explanation:
     * Choice B is correct because Passage 2's central concern is that UBI as a cash transfer may come at the expense of public services that deliver healthcare, childcare, and housing — services that markets do not adequately provide and that cash alone cannot reliably purchase, making the risk one of trading institutional provision for individual purchasing power.

     False Answer Explanations:
     * Choice A is incorrect because Passage 2 does not question the adequacy of the transfer amount; its objection is structural — about what cash transfers can and cannot provide relative to direct services.
     * Choice C is incorrect because Passage 2 does not raise inflation as a concern; its argument is about what social goods cash transfers can and cannot secure.
     * Choice D is incorrect because Passage 2 does not raise the work-disincentive concern, which is a separate critique of UBI not present in either passage.

     QUESTION 29
     Passage 1:
     The widespread adoption of open-plan office design was driven by genuine beliefs about collaboration and creativity. Removing walls between desks, the theory went, would increase spontaneous interaction, accelerate information sharing, and break down hierarchical barriers between employees and managers. Companies that made the switch reported feeling more dynamic and open.

     Passage 2:
     The research on open-plan offices tells a different story than the original theory predicted. Studies using sensor data have found that when companies removed private offices, face-to-face interaction actually declined — employees turned to headphones and screens to create private space, and communication shifted to email and messaging rather than conversation. The design intended to foster spontaneous collaboration produced the opposite: more isolation and more mediated interaction.

     Prompt:
     Based on the texts, which of the following best describes the relationship between the theory described in Passage 1 and the findings described in Passage 2?

     Options:
     A) The findings confirmed the theory by showing increased collaboration in open-plan offices.
     B) The findings revealed that the theory's predicted outcomes did not materialize and were in some cases reversed.
     C) The findings partially supported the theory but suggested that the design needed refinement.
     D) The two passages address different aspects of office design and do not bear on each other's claims.

     Correct Answer: B

     Correct Answer Explanation:
     * Choice B is correct because Passage 2 describes research showing that open-plan design produced less face-to-face interaction, not more — the opposite of what the theory in Passage 1 predicted — making the findings a reversal rather than a confirmation or partial confirmation.

     False Answer Explanations:
     * Choice A is incorrect because Passage 2's findings showed a decrease in face-to-face interaction, directly contradicting the theory rather than confirming it.
     * Choice C is incorrect because Passage 2 does not suggest partial support or refinement — the effect went in the opposite direction, which is a more fundamental contradiction than a need for adjustment.
     * Choice D is incorrect because both passages address open-plan office design and its effects on collaboration; they speak directly to the same question.

     QUESTION 30
     Passage 1:
     Philosopher Peter Singer argues that geographic and social distance are morally irrelevant when we are deciding whether to help those in need. If we would not walk past a drowning child in a shallow pond to avoid ruining our clothes, we should not fail to donate to programs that save lives overseas simply because we cannot see the suffering. The same moral logic applies: proximity does not change our obligation.

     Passage 2:
     Critics of Singer's argument point out that it makes no distinction between the special obligations we have to those near to us — family, community, neighbors — and obligations to distant strangers. Yet most ethical traditions recognize that our moral duties are not uniform: they are structured by relationships, roles, and reciprocity. A parent who gave equally to all children — their own and distant strangers — would be failing as a parent, not achieving moral perfection.

     Prompt:
     Based on the texts, the central disagreement between Singer's position and the critics described in Passage 2 is whether:

     Options:
     A) Effective aid organizations can reliably deliver assistance to people in need
     B) Moral obligations are uniform across all relationships and distances, or are legitimately structured by proximity and relational roles
     C) Wealth inequality between rich and poor countries is a serious ethical problem
     D) Donating to charity is a more effective way to help others than direct personal intervention

     Correct Answer: B

     Correct Answer Explanation:
     * Choice B is correct because Singer's argument, as described in Passage 1, holds that distance and relationship are morally irrelevant — obligation applies equally to the drowning child nearby and the distant stranger — while the critics in Passage 2 argue that ethical traditions correctly recognize that relational roles create differentiated obligations, making proximity and relationship morally relevant.

     False Answer Explanations:
     * Choice A is incorrect because neither passage raises the question of whether aid organizations are reliable; the dispute is philosophical, about the structure of moral obligation.
     * Choice C is incorrect because neither passage disputes whether global poverty is an ethical problem — Singer's argument assumes it is, and the critics do not challenge that premise.
     * Choice D is incorrect because neither passage draws a comparison between charity and direct intervention as methods; the dispute is about whether obligations to distant strangers exist at the same level as obligations to those nearby.

     [DIFFICULTY LEVEL: HIGH - QUESTIONS 31-40]

     QUESTION 31
     Passage 1:
     The literary scholar argues that the canon — the selection of texts granted the status of great literature — is never simply a record of aesthetic achievement. It is an act of cultural politics. The texts that have been elevated to canonical status in Western literary tradition share, with suspicious regularity, the perspectives of educated European men. This consistency is not coincidental: it reflects the institutional forces — universities, publishers, critics — that determined which voices were worth preserving and which were not.

     Passage 2:
     To reduce the literary canon to an exercise of power is to misunderstand how aesthetic judgment works. The texts that have endured across centuries and cultures have done so because they address, with unusual depth and skill, questions that persist across historical contexts: mortality, justice, love, the limits of knowledge. The fact that many canonical authors are European men reflects historical contingencies of literacy and publication, not a conspiracy to exclude. Works from non-Western traditions have entered the canon as institutions opened — suggesting the process is more self-correcting than the power critique allows.

     Prompt:
     Based on the texts, the author of Passage 2 would most likely respond to Passage 1's claim about "institutional forces" by arguing that:

     Options:
     A) Institutional forces have no effect on which texts are preserved or studied
     B) The longevity and cross-cultural appeal of canonical texts suggests their elevation reflects more than institutional bias alone
     C) European male authors are in fact superior writers to those from other traditions
     D) The literary canon should be expanded to include non-Western texts without removing any existing works

     Correct Answer: B

     Correct Answer Explanation:
     * Choice B is correct because Passage 2 argues that canonical texts have endured across centuries and cultures — an argument that institutional forces alone cannot explain — and notes that non-Western works have entered the canon as institutions opened, suggesting the canon is not a pure exercise of power but reflects genuine literary qualities as well.

     False Answer Explanations:
     * Choice A is incorrect because Passage 2 acknowledges historical contingencies of literacy and publication, implicitly granting that institutions had some effect — it argues that effect is not the whole story, not that it is nonexistent.
     * Choice C is incorrect because Passage 2 does not make any claim of inherent superiority based on cultural origin — its argument is about the universality of certain themes and the self-correcting nature of canonical processes.
     * Choice D is incorrect because Passage 2 does not make a specific policy recommendation about what should be added or removed; its argument is about how aesthetic judgment works, not about curriculum design.

     -----------------------------------------------------------------

     QUESTION 32
     Passage 1:
     Sociologist Erving Goffman argued that everyday social life is best understood through the metaphor of theatrical performance. People present carefully managed versions of themselves to others — their "front stage" behavior — while reserving a truer, less managed self for "back stage" contexts such as private relationships and moments of solitude. Social interaction is not a meeting of authentic selves but a negotiated performance in which impression management is constant and unavoidable.

     Passage 2:
     Goffman's theatrical model captures something important about social interaction but overstates its universality. The sharp division between a performed front stage and an authentic back stage assumes a stable core self that exists prior to and independent of social performance. But if the self is itself constituted through relational performance — as much of subsequent social theory has argued — then the back stage is not a refuge of authenticity but simply a different performance context with different norms and different audiences.

     Prompt:
     Based on the texts, the author of Passage 2 accepts which aspect of Goffman's theory while challenging another?

     Options:
     A) Accepts that impression management occurs in social interaction; challenges the assumption that a pre-social authentic self exists behind it
     B) Accepts that the self is stable and pre-social; challenges Goffman's claim that performances are unavoidable
     C) Accepts that back-stage behavior is authentic; challenges whether front-stage performance is truly managed
     D) Accepts Goffman's theatrical metaphor as fully adequate; challenges only its application to non-Western cultures

     Correct Answer: A

     Correct Answer Explanation:
     * Choice A is correct because Passage 2 grants that Goffman "captures something important about social interaction" — acknowledging the reality of performance and impression management — but then challenges the underlying assumption of a stable, pre-social core self, arguing that the self may be constituted through performance rather than merely expressed or concealed by it.

     False Answer Explanations:
     * Choice B is incorrect because Passage 2 argues the opposite regarding the self — it challenges the assumption that the self is stable and pre-social, which is precisely what Passage 2 disputes.
     * Choice C is incorrect because Passage 2 explicitly challenges the front/back-stage division by arguing that back-stage contexts are themselves performance contexts, not refuges of authentic non-performance.
     * Choice D is incorrect because Passage 2 does not endorse the theatrical metaphor as fully adequate — it calls the model an overstatement — and does not raise non-Western cultural contexts at all.

     -----------------------------------------------------------------

     QUESTION 33
     Passage 1:
     Linguist Noam Chomsky's theory of universal grammar holds that human beings are born with an innate capacity for language — a set of abstract structural principles common to all human languages that children use to acquire their native tongue. The speed and uniformity with which children across all cultures acquire language, despite limited and often imperfect input, argues for a biological foundation that cannot be explained by learning alone.

     Passage 2:
     The universalist claims of Chomsky's theory have come under increasing pressure from the study of linguistic diversity. Languages vary more radically in their structural properties than universal grammar predicts — some lack features the theory designates as universal. Researchers working in the usage-based tradition argue that children acquire language through domain-general cognitive capacities — pattern recognition, social learning, and statistical inference — without requiring a dedicated linguistic module. The data from cross-linguistic diversity suggests the universal grammar framework is more a product of its limited empirical base than a window onto innate cognitive architecture.

     Prompt:
     Based on the texts, the author of Passage 2 would most likely argue that the evidence cited in Passage 1 for universal grammar is:

     Options:
     A) Conclusive proof that an innate language faculty exists in all human beings
     B) Consistent with Chomsky's theory but equally consistent with domain-general cognitive explanations, and therefore not uniquely supportive of innate grammar
     C) Based on fabricated data that does not reflect actual patterns of child language acquisition
     D) Relevant only to Indo-European languages and inapplicable to non-Western linguistic traditions

     Correct Answer: B

     Correct Answer Explanation:
     * Choice B is correct because Passage 2 proposes that domain-general capacities — pattern recognition, statistical inference, social learning — can account for the speed and uniformity of language acquisition that Passage 1 treats as evidence for an innate module; this means the evidence is compatible with both theories, making it less conclusive than Passage 1 implies.

     False Answer Explanations:
     * Choice A is incorrect because Passage 2 directly challenges the universal grammar framework and would not regard any evidence as conclusive proof of it.
     * Choice C is incorrect because Passage 2 does not accuse Chomsky's supporting evidence of being fabricated; it questions the theory's framework and its empirical scope, not the integrity of the data.
     * Choice D is incorrect because Passage 2 mentions cross-linguistic diversity from multiple traditions as problematic for universal grammar, not as a reason to restrict the theory's geographic scope.

     -----------------------------------------------------------------

     QUESTION 34
     Passage 1:
     Historian Dipesh Chakrabarty argues that the discipline of history is structured around assumptions of secular, rational time — a linear progression in which the past is categorically different from the present and the supernatural has no causal role. This framework, he contends, emerged from a specifically European Enlightenment tradition and is ill-equipped to account for the experiences and self-understandings of people for whom ancestors, spirits, and the sacred actively shape present-day life. To write subaltern history fully, historians must work at the limits of their discipline.

     Passage 2:
     The call to expand history beyond secular rationalist frameworks, while well-intentioned, risks abandoning the critical tools that make historical inquiry distinct from other forms of cultural representation. The discipline's commitment to evidence, skepticism, and the falsifiability of claims about the past is not merely a Eurocentric imposition — it is what allows historians to challenge official narratives, expose state falsifications, and recover marginalized voices from the archive. Relativizing the epistemological foundations of history may serve no one less than the very subaltern subjects the critique seeks to protect.

     Prompt:
     Based on the texts, the author of Passage 2 would most likely argue that Chakrabarty's position, despite its stated intentions, risks:

     Options:
     A) Reinforcing Eurocentric narratives by focusing too narrowly on secular rationalism
     B) Undermining the methodological foundations that enable historians to challenge power and recover marginalized voices
     C) Overemphasizing the role of religion in non-Western societies
     D) Reducing historical inquiry to a form of literary criticism

     Correct Answer: B

     Correct Answer Explanation:
     * Choice B is correct because Passage 2 argues that the epistemological commitments Chakrabarty wants to loosen — evidence, skepticism, falsifiability — are precisely the tools that allow historians to challenge power and recover subaltern voices. Abandoning them, Passage 2 suggests, may harm the very people Chakrabarty seeks to help.

     False Answer Explanations:
     * Choice A is incorrect because Passage 2 defends secular rationalist methods, not as Eurocentric impositions, but as critical tools — and argues Chakrabarty's critique may inadvertently weaken rather than challenge those frameworks' hold.
     * Choice C is incorrect because Passage 2 does not address how non-Western societies are characterized; its concern is epistemological, not ethnographic.
     * Choice D is incorrect because while Passage 2 is concerned about the discipline's integrity, it does not specifically invoke the comparison to literary criticism; its argument is about evidence-based inquiry and political consequences.

     -----------------------------------------------------------------

     QUESTION 35
     Passage 1:
     The philosopher argues that moral progress is real and follows a recognizable pattern: the circle of moral concern expands over time to include beings and groups previously excluded. The abolition of slavery, the extension of political rights to women and formerly colonized peoples, and the growing recognition of animal suffering all trace this arc. What drives moral progress is not changing circumstances but the pressure of philosophical argument — reason forcing us to confront the inconsistencies in our existing moral commitments.

     Passage 2:
     The narrative of moral progress as expanding circles of reason-driven concern is too tidy. Historical expansions of rights were rarely the product of philosophical argument alone — they emerged from the political struggles, material interests, and contingent alliances of social movements. The abolition of slavery, for example, was as much the product of enslaved people's resistance, economic shifts, and geopolitical calculation as it was of abolitionist philosophy. Attributing moral change primarily to reason risks flattering the role of intellectuals while obscuring the messy, interest-laden processes through which it actually occurs.

     Prompt:
     Based on the texts, the central disagreement between the two authors concerns:

     Options:
     A) Whether slavery was morally wrong
     B) Whether moral progress has occurred at all in human history
     C) Whether philosophical reasoning is the primary driver of moral progress, or whether material and political forces play a more fundamental role
     D) Whether the expansion of voting rights represents genuine moral progress

     Correct Answer: C

     Correct Answer Explanation:
     * Choice C is correct because Passage 1 attributes moral progress primarily to philosophical argument and the pressure of reason on existing moral commitments, while Passage 2 argues that historical changes in rights and moral status were driven at least as much by political struggle, material interests, and contingent alliances as by philosophical reasoning — the disagreement is about the mechanism and primary driver of moral change.

     False Answer Explanations:
     * Choice A is incorrect because neither author disputes that slavery was wrong; it is treated as a clear example of historical moral progress by both, and the debate is about how that change came about.
     * Choice B is incorrect because Passage 2 does not deny that moral progress has occurred — it challenges the explanation for how it happens, not whether it happens.
     * Choice D is incorrect because neither author questions whether extending voting rights was morally progressive; the example is used to illustrate a point about mechanism, not to dispute the value of the change itself.

     -----------------------------------------------------------------

     QUESTION 36
     Passage 1:
     Cognitive scientist Andy Clark argues that the mind does not stop at the skull. When we use tools — a notebook, a smartphone, a map — to store, process, and manipulate information, those external systems become part of the cognitive process itself. The extended mind thesis holds that cognition is not confined to internal neural processes but is distributed across brain, body, and environment. What makes something a mental state is its functional role in guiding behavior, not its physical location.

     Passage 2:
     The extended mind thesis dissolves an important distinction. There is a meaningful difference between a belief I hold and a note I wrote to myself last Tuesday — even if both guide my behavior. The note can be lost, stolen, altered, or read by others; the belief cannot be. Internal mental states have properties — privacy, immediacy, and a particular relationship to consciousness and agency — that external artifacts lack. To call both equally cognitive is to use a functional criterion so broad it obscures rather than illuminates what is distinctive about minds.

     Prompt:
     Based on the texts, the author of Passage 2 accepts which premise of the extended mind thesis while rejecting its conclusion?

     Options:
     A) Accepts that external tools can guide behavior; rejects that functional role in behavior is a sufficient criterion for mental-state status
     B) Accepts that the mind is entirely confined to the skull; rejects the claim that tools affect cognition in any way
     C) Accepts that internal neural processes are insufficient for cognition; rejects the claim that tools are ever useful
     D) Accepts that consciousness is distributed across the environment; rejects the claim that this has philosophical implications

     Correct Answer: A

     Correct Answer Explanation:
     * Choice A is correct because Passage 2 implicitly grants that external tools like notebooks guide behavior — the note guides behavior "last Tuesday" — but argues that functional role in guiding behavior is not a sufficient criterion for counting something as a mental state, because internal states have properties (privacy, immediacy, relationship to consciousness) that external artifacts lack.

     False Answer Explanations:
     * Choice B is incorrect because Passage 2 does not claim the mind is entirely brain-bound or deny that tools affect cognition — its objection is conceptual, about the definition of mental states, not empirical.
     * Choice C is incorrect because Passage 2 does not claim that internal processes alone are insufficient for cognition; it defends the importance of internal states against the extended mind's functional criterion.
     * Choice D is incorrect because Passage 2 does not accept that consciousness is distributed — that claim is closer to what Clark argues in Passage 1.

     QUESTION 37
     Passage 1:
     The political theorist argues that deliberative democracy — a model in which legitimate political decisions emerge from inclusive, reason-giving public discourse — rests on an idealized and exclusionary conception of rational argument. What counts as a "reasonable" contribution to political deliberation is itself shaped by class, education, and cultural capital. Forms of expression that fall outside the norms of formal argumentation — testimony, narrative, emotional speech — are systematically devalued in deliberative theory, effectively silencing those whose political experience cannot be easily translated into the idiom the theory demands.

     Passage 2:
     The critique of deliberative democracy as exclusionary does not follow from its premises. Deliberative theory, properly understood, does not demand that participants speak in formal philosophical prose — it requires only that contributions be offered in good faith and with openness to the force of better reasons. A personal narrative that conveys injustice can be a legitimate deliberative contribution if it expands the shared understanding from which collective decisions are made. The problem is not deliberation per se but the impoverished institutional settings in which it is often practiced.

     Prompt:
     Based on the texts, the author of Passage 2 would most likely argue that the author of Passage 1 has:

     Options:
     A) Correctly identified a flaw inherent in deliberative democratic theory that cannot be remedied
     B) Confused the theory's requirements with the inadequate institutional forms through which deliberation is often practiced
     C) Underestimated the degree to which formal argumentation dominates all political contexts
     D) Misattributed the exclusionary effects of deliberation to class rather than to race and gender

     Correct Answer: B

     Correct Answer Explanation:
     * Choice B is correct because Passage 2 explicitly distinguishes between deliberative theory's actual requirements — good faith and openness to better reasons — and the "impoverished institutional settings" in which deliberation is practiced, arguing that Passage 1 has attributed to the theory a fault that belongs to its institutional implementation.

     False Answer Explanations:
     * Choice A is incorrect because Passage 2 rejects the idea that the exclusionary problem is inherent in deliberative theory — it argues the theory, properly understood, accommodates narrative and emotional speech.
     * Choice C is incorrect because Passage 2 acknowledges that institutional settings can be impoverished but does not argue formal argumentation dominates all political contexts; its point is that the theory does not require formal argumentation.
     * Choice D is incorrect because Passage 2 does not dispute Passage 1's identification of class and cultural capital as relevant factors — it disputes the claim that those factors are inherent to deliberative theory rather than to its institutional practice.

     -----------------------------------------------------------------

     QUESTION 38
     Passage 1:
     Economist Joseph Schumpeter's concept of "creative destruction" describes capitalism's tendency to generate economic growth by continuously dismantling existing industries and firms as new technologies and competitors render them obsolete. For Schumpeter, this disruption was not a flaw in the system but its essential virtue: the mechanism through which resources flow to more productive uses and living standards rise over the long run.

     Passage 2:
     Schumpeter's framing of creative destruction as a virtue depends on a level of abstraction that erases the human costs of the process. The "long run" in which resources flow to more productive uses is not lived by the workers whose skills are made redundant, the communities whose economic base is destroyed, or the small businesses that cannot survive the predatory entry of well-capitalized competitors. What looks like efficient reallocation from a macroeconomic vantage point looks like devastation from the level of actual human experience.

     Prompt:
     Based on the texts, the author of Passage 2 would most likely argue that Schumpeter's analysis is flawed because it:

     Options:
     A) Fails to identify the role of technology in driving economic change
     B) Privileges an aggregate macroeconomic perspective in ways that render the unequal distribution of costs and benefits invisible
     C) Overestimates the speed at which new industries replace those that are destroyed
     D) Incorrectly predicts that living standards will rise under capitalism

     Correct Answer: B

     Correct Answer Explanation:
     * Choice B is correct because Passage 2's central critique is that Schumpeter's macroeconomic framing — resources flowing to more productive uses, living standards rising over the long run — operates at a level of abstraction that makes the concentrated costs borne by specific workers, communities, and small businesses invisible while the diffuse aggregate benefits remain visible.

     False Answer Explanations:
     * Choice A is incorrect because Passage 2 does not dispute Schumpeter's account of technology's role in driving change; its objection is ethical and perspectival, not empirical.
     * Choice C is incorrect because Passage 2 does not address the pace of industrial replacement; it focuses on who bears the costs of disruption, regardless of how quickly it occurs.
     * Choice D is incorrect because Passage 2 does not dispute that aggregate living standards may rise in the long run — it disputes whether that aggregate outcome justifies or adequately describes the concentrated harms experienced along the way.
